<span class="italic"><a title="Link to journal home page" href="https://doi.org/10.1039/2040-3372/2009">Nanoscale</a></span>, 2024, <strong>16</strong>, 15699-15712</div><h1 id="sect180"><span class="title_heading">Hexagonal Mg<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> and Ca<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> monolayers as promising anode materials for Li-ion and Na-ion batteries<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a></span></h1><p class="header_text">

      <br><span class="italic">School of Materials Science and Engineering, Beihang University, Beijing 100191, China. E-mail: <a href="mailto:jzhou@buaa.edu.cn">jzhou@buaa.edu.cn</a>; <a href="mailto:zmsun@buaa.edu.cn">zmsun@buaa.edu.cn</a></span>
    </p><div id="art-admin"><span class="italic bold">Received 
      25th April 2024
    </span><span class="bold italic">, Accepted 29th July 2024</span></div><p class="bold italic">First published on 5th August 2024</p><hr><div class="abstract"><h2>Abstract</h2><p>Exploring new high-performance anode materials is of great significance for next generation renewable energy technologies. Two-dimensional (2D) transition metal borides, named as MBenes, are receiving increasing attention in energy storage fields due to their structure-and-composition diversities and high electrical conductivities. Expanding the family of hexagonal MBenes to non-transition metal borides, we report two hexagonal IIA metal M<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small>-typed MBenes and demonstrate the feasibility of exfoliation and application as anode materials for Li-ion batteries (LIBs) and Na-ion batteries (SIBs) using <span class="italic">ab initio</span> calculations. The predicted 2D Mg<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> and Ca<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> monolayers exhibit robust thermal and mechanical stabilities, inherent metallic properties, electrical conductivities, and excellent electrode performance. The outstanding theoretical specific capacities of Mg<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> and Ca<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> for Li/Na ions reach up to 764/527 and 859/527 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small>, respectively. Furthermore, the average open-circuit voltages (OCVs) of Mg<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> and Ca<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> range from 0.275 to 0.395 V, indicating their suitability for anode materials. Additionally, the low diffusion energy barriers of Mg<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> and Ca<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> for Li/Na ions at delithiated/desodiated states (27/14 and 16/12 meV) and lithiated/sodiated states (187/58 and 52/34 meV) illustrate their excellent chargedischarge capabilities. These intriguing findings demonstrate the superior performance of the predicted IIA metal boride monolayers as promising anode materials for LIBs and SIBs and break through the limitations of transition metal MBenes as anode materials in terms of overall performance. Our study opens the door to the application of the main group metal MBenes in ion energy storage.</p></div><hr>
    
      
      <h2 id="sect264"><span class="a_heading">1. Introduction</span></h2>
      <span>Energy storage technology is of great significance to the development of society.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit1"><sup><span class="sup_ref">1</span></sup></a> Rechargeable LIBs have garnered increasing attention as an effective and clean energy storage technology due to their reversible capacity, high power density, portability, and long cycle life.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit2"><sup><span class="sup_ref">24</span></sup></a> At present, LIBs are widely used in portable electronics, electric vehicles, and power grid systems.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit5"><sup><span class="sup_ref">5,6</span></sup></a> In addition to LIBs, NIBs with abundant reserves and high safety are promising options for rechargeable batteries in the future.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit7"><sup><span class="sup_ref">79</span></sup></a> The performances of LIBs and NIBs are highly dependent on the properties of electrode materials. Graphite is widely used as an anode material for LIBs due to the high cycle stability and Coulomb efficiency, but the low specific capacity (372 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small>) and high diffusion energy barrier (4501200 meV) limit its further application.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit10"><sup><span class="sup_ref">10</span></sup></a> In addition, graphite is not suitable for NIBs because Na ions are unable to form a robust interaction with it. Hence, there is an urgent need for new anode materials to enhance the performance of LIBs and NIBs. In recent years, many Two-dimensional (2D) materials have been investigated as anode materials in metal-ion batteries due to their high specific surface area, high electrical conductivity and good mechanical properties.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit11"><sup><span class="sup_ref">1117</span></sup></a> Graphene is the first example of 2D electrode materials for LIBs.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit18"><sup><span class="sup_ref">18</span></sup></a> Other 2D materials such as transition metal oxides (TMOs),<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit19"><sup><span class="sup_ref">1921</span></sup></a> transition metal dichalcogenides (TMDs),<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit22"><sup><span class="sup_ref">2226</span></sup></a> and transition metal carbides and nitrides (MXenes)<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit12"><sup><span class="sup_ref">12,13,2729</span></sup></a> were also investigated as anode materials for LIBs or NIBs. Recently, MBenes<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit30"><sup><span class="sup_ref">30</span></sup></a> have garnered increasing attention because of their high performances as anode materials for metal-ion batteries. Notably, in 2017, Guo <span class="italic">et al.</span> first reported the orthorhombic MBenes,<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit30"><sup><span class="sup_ref">30</span></sup></a> including Fe<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> and Mo<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small>, which exhibited high storage capacities (665 and 444 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small>) for LIBs. Since then, a series of MBenes were investigated as anode materials for metal-ion batteries.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit16"><sup><span class="sup_ref">16,3033</span></sup></a> The hexagonal Sc<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small>,<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit34"><sup><span class="sup_ref">34</span></sup></a> Ti<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small>,<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit35"><sup><span class="sup_ref">35</span></sup></a> Zr<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small><a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit36"><sup><span class="sup_ref">36</span></sup></a> monolayers possess ultra-low diffusion energy barriers (3, 17 and 17 meV), while their specific capacities are relatively low (480, 456 and 526 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small>) for LIBs. For NIBs, the hexagonal V<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small><a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit37"><sup><span class="sup_ref">37</span></sup></a> demonstrate a quite high specific capacity (814 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small>). However, the high initial and average voltages (1.2 and 0.7 V) reduce the advantages of it as anode materials. Additionally, the hexagonal Ti<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small><a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit35"><sup><span class="sup_ref">35</span></sup></a> has an ultra-low diffusion energy barrier (8 meV) but a commonplace specific capacity (342 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small>) for Na ions. Although MBenes show the potential as anode materials due to their high specific capacities and low ion diffusion energy barriers, there is still room for improving their overall performances. It is necessary and meaningful to find new anode materials with high performances for LIBs and NIBs.</span>
      <p class="otherpara">MBenes was defined as a family of 2D transition metal borides with sandwich-like structures which are derived from bulk ternary layered transition metal borides (MABs).<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit31"><sup><span class="sup_ref">31</span></sup></a> At present, the MBene family only covers transition metal borides, among which the most reported are the orthogonal and hexagonal M<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small>-type MBenes (M = Sc, Ti, V, Cr, Mn, Fe, Co, Ni, Y, Zr, Nb, Mo, Tc, Ru, Rh, Hf, Ta and W).<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit31"><sup><span class="sup_ref">31</span></sup></a> Actually, the hexagonal bulk binary layered metal borides (MB<small><sub>2</sub></small>) are also potential precursors for hexagonal MBenes apart from hexagonal MABs.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit32"><sup><span class="sup_ref">32</span></sup></a> The hexagonal bulk MB<small><sub>2</sub></small> is composed of alternating layers of metal atoms and honeycomb boron rings. In experiments, the 2D MB<small><sub>2</sub></small> (M = Ti, Zr, Hf, Nb, Ta, Cr, Al and Mg) nanosheets can be prepared by liquid-phase exfoliation of bulk MB<small><sub>2</sub></small>.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit38"><sup><span class="sup_ref">38,39</span></sup></a> Nonetheless, to obtain their monolayer structures, refinements in experimental methods and solvents are essential.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit33"><sup><span class="sup_ref">33</span></sup></a> Theoretically, it is possible to obtain two types of monolayer structures from bulk MB<small><sub>2</sub></small>. One is the MB<small><sub>2</sub></small> monolayer (MB<small><sub>2</sub></small>) composed of a layer of metal atoms and a layer of boron rings. The other is the M<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> monolayer (MB<small><sub>2</sub></small>M), which consists of two layers of metal atoms sandwiching a layer of boron rings, with the same structure as the hexagonal M<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small>-type MBene. Currently, some hexagonal MB<small><sub>2</sub></small> (M = Ti, Hf, V, Nb, Ta, Fe, Be and Mg) monolayers have been verified to be stable by <span class="italic">ab initio</span> simulations, which cover IIA, IIIA and transition metal borides.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit40"><sup><span class="sup_ref">40</span></sup></a> However, there is a lack of research on M<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> monolayers originating from bulk MB<small><sub>2</sub></small>, especially for main group metal borides.</p>
      <p class="otherpara">To enhance the specific capacity of MBene anode materials, efforts should be made to increase the ion loading capacity and reduce the molecular weight. To decrease the OCV of MBene anode materials, it is necessary to reduce the variation of energy with ion concentration during charging or discharging, which requires MBene to have a moderate adsorption energy for Li/Na ions. Currently, transition metal element MBenes possess high molecular weights and low adsorption energies for Li/Na ions, which respectively limit the enhancement of specific capacity and the decrease of OCV. If new MBenes with low molecular weights and moderate ion adsorption energies can be selected from non-transition metal elements, the overall electrode performance of MBenes will be further improved.</p>
      <p class="otherpara">In this work, we investigated the hexagonal IIIIA metal M<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> monolayers by <span class="italic">ab initio</span> calculations aiming to expand the MBene family to non-transition metal borides. Initially, we identified the stabilities of bulk MB<small><sub>2</sub></small> and corresponding M<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> monolayers (M = Li, Na, Mg, Ca, Al, Ga, In, Tl) at 0K. Subsequently, we investigated the exfoliation of these M<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> monolayers from bulk MB<small><sub>2</sub></small> and compared them to that of MB<small><sub>2</sub></small> monolayers. The results indicated that M<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> monolayers are more easily exfoliated from bulk MB<small><sub>2</sub></small> than MB<small><sub>2</sub></small> monolayers based on the surface formation energies. Finally, motivated by the excellent performance of MBenes in metal-ion battery anodes, we chose Mg<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small>, Ca<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> and Al<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> to explore their applications in LIBs and SIBs. Specifically, the Mg<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> and Ca<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> monolayers were predicted to be promising for anode materials in LIBs and SIBs, with good thermal stabilities, high specific capacities, low average OCVs and low diffusion energy barriers. In conclusion, the IIA metal MBenes hold promise in enhancing the performance of anode materials for LIBs and NIBs, which is crucial for driving the innovation and advancement in energy storage technologies.</p>
    
    
      
      <h2 id="sect358"><span class="a_heading">2. Computational methods</span></h2>
      <span>The <span class="italic">ab initio</span> calculations were performed using the Density Functional Theory (DFT) method as implemented in the Vienna <span class="italic">Ab initio</span> Simulation Package (VASP).<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit41"><sup><span class="sup_ref">41</span></sup></a> The exchangecorrelation interactions were described using the PerdewBurkeErnzerh (PBE) functional of the generalized gradient approximation.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit42"><sup><span class="sup_ref">42</span></sup></a> The plane-wave cutoff energy of 520 eV was used for all the calculations. For the structure optimizations, the force and energy convergence criteria were set to 1  10<small><sup>4</sup></small> eV <small><sup>1</sup></small> and 1  10<small><sup>5</sup></small> eV, respectively, and the <span class="italic">k</span> meshes were set to 4  4  1 using the MonkhorstPack method.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit43"><sup><span class="sup_ref">43</span></sup></a> For the static calculations, we adopted the <span class="italic">k</span> meshes of 6  6  1. For band and density of state calculations, the PBE and HSE06 functionals were respectively adopted to study the effect of the exchangecorrelation functional on electronic properties of MBenes. The van der Waals (vdW) interaction in 2D MBenes was corrected by the DFT-D3 (BeckeJohnson damping) method.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit44"><sup><span class="sup_ref">44</span></sup></a> To avoid the interlayer interactions, a vacuum spacing of 20  was used in the <span class="italic">z</span> direction. The phonon calculations were performed based on the DFPT (density-functional perturbation theory)<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit45"><sup><span class="sup_ref">45,46</span></sup></a> implemented in VASP with the 2  2  1 supercells for MBenes and 2  2  2 supercells for bulk MB<small><sub>2</sub></small>. The phonon spectra were obtained using the PHONOPY codes.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit47"><sup><span class="sup_ref">47</span></sup></a> The <span class="italic">ab initio</span> molecular dynamics (AIMD) simulations employed the canonical ensembles (NVT) controlling the temperatures by the algorithm of Nos.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit48"><sup><span class="sup_ref">48</span></sup></a> For MBenes, we used the 5  5  1 supercells in the AIMD simulations. The climbing image nudged elastic band method (CI-NEB)<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit49"><sup><span class="sup_ref">49,50</span></sup></a> was adopted to determine the transition states and calculate the diffusion energy barriers of Li/Na. The surface formation energies of M<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> and MB<small><sub>2</sub></small> monolayers are calculated using the following equation,<table><tbody><tr><td class="leftEqn">&nbsp;</td></tr><tr><td class="eqn"><span id="eqn1"><span id="eqn1"><span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>f-slab-M<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small></sub></small> = (<span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>slab-M<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small></sub></small>  <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>bulk-MB<small><sub>2</sub></small></sub></small>  <span class="italic"></span><small><sub>M</sub></small>)/(2<span class="italic">S</span>)</span></span></td><td class="rightEqn">(1)</td></tr></tbody></table>where <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>slab-M<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small></sub></small> and <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>bulk-MB<small><sub>2</sub></small></sub></small> represent the total energies of M<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> monolayers and bulk MB<small><sub>2</sub></small>, respectively. <span class="italic"></span><small><sub>M</sub></small> represents the energy of the bulk M metal per atom. <span class="italic">S</span> is the cross-section area of bulk MB<small><sub>2</sub></small>.<table><tbody><tr><td class="leftEqn">&nbsp;</td></tr><tr><td class="eqn"><span id="eqn2"><span id="eqn2"><span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>f-slab-MB<small><sub>2</sub></small></sub></small> = (<span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>slab-MB<small><sub>2</sub></small></sub></small>  <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>bulk-MB<small><sub>2</sub></small></sub></small>)/(2<span class="italic">S</span>)</span></span></td><td class="rightEqn">(2)</td></tr></tbody></table>where <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>slab-MB<small><sub>2</sub></small></sub></small> and <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>bulk-MB<small><sub>2</sub></small></sub></small> represent the total energies of MB<small><sub>2</sub></small> monolayers and bulk MB<small><sub>2</sub></small>, respectively. <span class="italic">S</span> is the cross-section area of bulk MB<small><sub>2</sub></small>. The single atom adsorption energies for Li and Na ions are calculated according to the following equation,<table><tbody><tr><td class="leftEqn">&nbsp;</td></tr><tr><td class="eqn"><span id="eqn3"><span id="eqn3"><span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>ad</sub></small> = (<span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>M<small><sub><span class="italic">n</span></sub></small>MBene</sub></small>  <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>MBene</sub></small>  <span class="italic">nE</span><small><sub>M</sub></small>)/<span class="italic">n</span>(M = Li, Na)</span></span></td><td class="rightEqn">(3)</td></tr></tbody></table>where <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>M<small><sub><span class="italic">n</span></sub></small>MBene</sub></small> and <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>MBene</sub></small> represent the total energies of the MBene adsorbed with <span class="italic">n</span> Li/Na ions and the isolated MBene, respectively. <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>M</sub></small> represents the energy of the body-centered cubic Li/Na per atom. <span class="italic">n</span> is the number of adsorbed Li/Na atoms, which equals 1 for a single atom. To evaluate the adsorption stabilities of the Li/Na layers on MBenes, the average adsorption energies are calculated according to the following equation,<table><tbody><tr><td class="leftEqn">&nbsp;</td></tr><tr><td class="eqn"><span id="eqn4"><span id="eqn4"><span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>ave</sub></small> = (<span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>M<small><sub><span class="italic">i</span></sub></small>MBene</sub></small>  <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>M<small><sub><span class="italic">i</span>1</sub></small>MBene</sub></small>  <span class="italic">mE</span><small><sub>M</sub></small>)/<span class="italic">m</span>(M = Li, Na)</span></span></td><td class="rightEqn">(4)</td></tr></tbody></table>where <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>M<small><sub><span class="italic">i</span></sub></small>MBene</sub></small> and <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>M<small><sub><span class="italic">i</span>1</sub></small>MBene</sub></small> represent the total energies of MBenes adsorbed with <span class="italic">i</span> and <span class="italic">i</span>  1 Li/Na layers, <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>M</sub></small> is the energy of the body-centered cubic Li/Na per atom and <span class="italic">m</span> is the number of adsorbed Li/Na ions in the <span class="italic">i</span> layer. The theoretical specific capacities are calculated by the following equation,<table><tbody><tr><td class="leftEqn">&nbsp;</td></tr><tr><td class="eqn"><span id="eqn5"><span id="eqn5"><span class="italic">C</span> = <span class="italic">nzF</span>/<span class="italic">M</span><small><sub>MBene</sub></small></span></span></td><td class="rightEqn">(5)</td></tr></tbody></table>where <span class="italic">n</span> stands the number of adsorbed Li/Na ions, <span class="italic">z</span> is the valence number of Li/Na, <span class="italic">F</span> represents the Faraday constant (26<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">801 mA h mol<small><sup>1</sup></small>) and <span class="italic">M</span><small><sub>MBene</sub></small> represents the molecular weight of the MBene. To assess the thermodynamic stability of the adsorption configurations, the formation energies are obtained from the following equation,<table><tbody><tr><td class="leftEqn">&nbsp;</td></tr><tr><td class="eqn"><span id="eqn6"><span id="eqn6"><span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>f</sub></small>(<span class="italic">x</span>) = (<span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>M<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>MBene</sub></small>  <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>MBene</sub></small>  <span class="italic">xE</span><small><sub>M</sub></small>)/(<span class="italic">x</span> + 1)(M = Li, Na)</span></span></td><td class="rightEqn">(6)</td></tr></tbody></table>where <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>M<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>MBene</sub></small> and <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>MBene</sub></small> represent the total energies of the MBene adsorbed with <span class="italic">x</span> Li/Na ions and the isolated MBene, respectively. <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>M</sub></small> represents the energy of the body-centered cubic Li/Na per atom. <span class="italic">x</span> is the number of adsorbed Li/Na atoms. The OCV with respect to M/M<small><sup>+</sup></small> between the adjacent ion concentrations <span class="italic">x</span><small><sub>1</sub></small> and <span class="italic">x</span><small><sub>2</sub></small> is calculated as the following equation,<table><tbody><tr><td class="leftEqn">&nbsp;</td></tr><tr><td class="eqn"><span id="eqn7"><a href="/image/article/2024/NR/d4nr01786b/d4nr01786b-t1_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d4nr01786b-t1.tif" src="/image/article/2024/NR/d4nr01786b/d4nr01786b-t1.gif"></a></span></td><td class="rightEqn">(7)</td></tr></tbody></table>where <span class="italic">E</span>(<span class="italic">x</span><small><sub>2</sub></small>) and <span class="italic">E</span>(<span class="italic">x</span><small><sub>1</sub></small>) represent the total energies of the adsorption configurations at Li/Na concentration <span class="italic">x</span><small><sub>1</sub></small> and <span class="italic">x</span><small><sub>2</sub></small>, respectively. <span class="italic">N</span><small><sub><span class="italic">x</span><small><sub>2</sub></small></sub></small> and <span class="italic">N</span><small><sub><span class="italic">x</span><small><sub>1</sub></small></sub></small> stand the number of adsorbed Li/Na ions at concentration <span class="italic">x</span><small><sub>1</sub></small> and <span class="italic">x</span><small><sub>2</sub></small>, respectively. <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>M</sub></small> represents the energy of the body-centered cubic Li/Na per atom. The diffusion coefficients of Li/Na ions are obtained from the following equation,<table><tbody><tr><td class="leftEqn">&nbsp;</td></tr><tr><td class="eqn"><span id="eqn8"><span id="eqn8"><span class="italic">D</span> = <span class="italic">l</span><small><sup>2</sup></small><span class="italic"></span><small><sub>0</sub></small><img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_2009.gif" alt="[thin space (1/6-em)]">exp(<span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>barrier</sub></small>/(<span class="italic">k</span><small><sub>B</sub></small><span class="italic">T</span>))</span></span></td><td class="rightEqn">(8)</td></tr></tbody></table>where <span class="italic">l</span> is the ion migration distance between the equivalent S1 sites on the MBenes, <span class="italic"></span><small><sub>0</sub></small> is the vibrational frequency, <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>barrier</sub></small> is the diffusion energy barrier, <span class="italic">k</span><small><sub>B</sub></small> presents the Boltzmann constant and <span class="italic">T</span> stands the temperature.</span>
    
    
      
      <h2 id="sect582"><span class="a_heading">3. Results and discussion</span></h2>
      
        
        <h3 id="sect585"><span class="b_heading">3.1 Stabilities and exfoliation of M<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> monolayers</span></h3>
        <span>The structures of hexagonal bulk MB<small><sub>2</sub></small>, MB<small><sub>2</sub></small> monolayer and M<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> monolayer are exhibited in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1</a>. These structures exhibit a high similarity, consisting of stacked metal atom layers and honeycomb boron ring layers. Inspired by the structural similarity of these systems and the elemental diversity of MB<small><sub>2</sub></small> covering transition metal and main group metal, we investigated the IIIIA metal M<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> monolayers by <span class="italic">ab initio</span> calculations to expand the hexagonal MBene family. Initially, we verified the dynamic stabilities of hexagonal bulk MB<small><sub>2</sub></small> (where M = Li, Na, K, Be, Mg, Ca, Al, Ga, In, Tl) through phonon calculations. The results in Fig. S1<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a> show that all IIIIA metal bulk MB<small><sub>2</sub></small> are dynamically stable except for the bulk KB<small><sub>2</sub></small>. Subsequently, we focused on screening new hexagonal IA, IIA and IIIA metal M<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> monolayers. We calculated the phonon spectra of M<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> monolayers for the same metal elements as MB<small><sub>2</sub></small> to investigate their dynamic stabilities. Except for the Be<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> monolayer, all these monolayers were verified to be dynamically stable without imaginary frequencies in their phonon spectra as displayed in Fig. S2.<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a> Interestingly, the bulk KB<small><sub>2</sub></small> is dynamically unstable whereas the K<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> monolayer is dynamically stable. Furthermore, we calculated the exfoliation energies of M<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> monolayers from bulk MB<small><sub>2</sub></small> and compared them to that of MB<small><sub>2</sub></small> monolayers. The detailed results are shown in Fig. S5 and Table S2.<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a> In Fig. S5,<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a> the exfoliation energies of stable IIIIA metal M<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> monolayers (0.301.37 J m<small><sup>2</sup></small>) are significantly lower than those of transition metal M<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> (M = Ti, Zr and Hf) monolayers (2.352.59 J m<small><sup>2</sup></small>), whose nanosheets have been exfoliated in experiments.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit38"><sup><span class="sup_ref">38</span></sup></a> In Table S2,<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a> the exfoliation energy values of M<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> and MB<small><sub>2</sub></small> monolayers are very close with a maximum difference no more than 0.001 J m<small><sup>2</sup></small>, indicating that both can potentially be exfoliated from bulk MB<small><sub>2</sub></small>. To determine the surface stability of M<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> and MB<small><sub>2</sub></small> monolayers, we calculated their surface formation energies using <a title="" href="#eqn1">eqn (1) and (2)</a>, respectively. Fig. S6 and Table S3<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a> shows that the surface formation energies of M<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> for all M elements are smaller than that of MB<small><sub>2</sub></small>, indicating that M<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> terminated by M atoms on both sides is more stable than MB<small><sub>2</sub></small> terminated by M atoms on only one side. These results demonstrate that the M<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> monolayers are more easily exfoliated from the bulk MB<small><sub>2</sub></small> than the MB<small><sub>2</sub></small> monolayers by breaking the MB bonds at the blue dashed line instead of the red dashed line as shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1</a>. Additionally, the surface formation energies of non-transition metal M<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> and MB<small><sub>2</sub></small> monolayers (0.0460.750 J m<small><sup>2</sup></small> and 0.2641.329 J m<small><sup>2</sup></small>) are smaller than that of transition metal systems such as Ti, Zr and Hf (1.1411.458 J m<small><sup>2</sup></small> and 0.2642.158 J m<small><sup>2</sup></small>). This indicates that non-transition metal monolayers require less energy to be obtained than that of transition metal monolayers.</span>
        <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig1"><a href="/image/article/2024/NR/d4nr01786b/d4nr01786b-f1_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d4nr01786b-f1.tif" src="/image/article/2024/NR/d4nr01786b/d4nr01786b-f1.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 1 </b> <span id="fig1"><span class="graphic_title">(a) Side and top views of hexagonal bulk MB<small><sub>2</sub></small> and (b) side views of hexagonal MB<small><sub>2</sub></small> and (c) M<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> monolayers (M = Li, Na, K, Be, Mg, Ca, Al, Ga, In, Tl). The purple and green balls represent the M and B atoms, respectively. All these systems possess the <span class="italic">P</span>6/<span class="italic">mmm</span> space group. The MB<small><sub>2</sub></small> or M<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> monolayers can be exfoliated from bulk MB<small><sub>2</sub></small> depending on whether the MB bonds break at the blue or red dashed line, respectively.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
        <p class="otherpara">Motivated by the applications of hexagonal MBenes in metal-ion battery anodes,<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit34"><sup><span class="sup_ref">3437,5153</span></sup></a> we investigated hexagonal non-transition metal M<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> systems for LIB and NIB anodes. Considering the stability and molecular weight, we selected the Mg<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> and Ca<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> monolayers as candidate anode materials for further investigation.</p>
      
      
        
        <h3 id="sect677"><span class="b_heading">3.2 Structures and stabilities of Mg<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> and Ca<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> monolayers</span></h3>
        <span>The optimized structures of hexagonal Mg<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> and Ca<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> monolayers with the <span class="italic">P</span>6/<span class="italic">mmm</span> space group are displayed in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2a and b</a>, respectively. The MM distances in planes are 3.078  and 3.333  and the thicknesses are 3.393  and 3.714  for Mg<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> and Ca<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small>, respectively. First of all, the previous calculated phonon spectra in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3a and b</a> show that the hexagonal Mg<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> and Ca<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> monolayers are dynamically stable without imaginary phonon frequencies in the whole Brillouin zones. Subsequently, we calculated the elastic constants (<span class="italic">c</span><small><sub><span class="italic">ij</span></sub></small>) of Mg<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> and Ca<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> to evaluate their mechanical stabilities. The results in Fig. S3 and Table S1 in the ESI<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a> demonstrate that these two 2D hexagonal crystals are mechanically stable satisfying <span class="italic">c</span><small><sub>11</sub></small> &gt; 0 and <span class="italic">c</span><small><sub>11</sub></small> &gt; |<span class="italic">c</span><small><sub>12</sub></small>|. Furthermore, in order to evaluate their thermal stabilities, we conducted AIMD simulations for 10 ps at 300, 600, 900, 1200 and 3000 K, respectively. At 300 K, the average values of the total energies remain almost constant throughout the simulations as depicted in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3c and d</a>. The corresponding structure snapshots show the intact geometries with only minor deformations in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3e and f</a>. These results indicate that the Mg<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> and Ca<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> monolayers remain stable at room temperature. The energy fluctuations and structure snapshots of AIMD simulations at other temperatures are presented in Fig. S7 and S8,<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a> respectively. The Mg<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> monolayer keeps stable until 1200 K indicating the high stability for the application in metal-ion battery anodes. The Ca<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> monolayer begins to tear and coalesce into chains at 900 K and the average of energies fluctuates drastically. Nonetheless, it remains stable at 600 K, which is enough for the application of anode materials.</span>
        <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig2"><a href="/image/article/2024/NR/d4nr01786b/d4nr01786b-f2_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d4nr01786b-f2.tif" src="/image/article/2024/NR/d4nr01786b/d4nr01786b-f2.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 2 </b> <span id="fig2"><span class="graphic_title">Side and top views of (a) Mg<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> and (b) Ca<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> monolayers. The orange, blue and green balls represent the Mg, Ca, and B atoms, respectively. The layer thicknesses and in-plane lattice constants are labeled in angstrom.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
        <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig3"><a href="/image/article/2024/NR/d4nr01786b/d4nr01786b-f3_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d4nr01786b-f3.tif" src="/image/article/2024/NR/d4nr01786b/d4nr01786b-f3.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 3 </b> <span id="fig3"><span class="graphic_title">Phonon spectra along the high symmetry directions for (a) Mg<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> and (b) Ca<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small>. Total energy fluctuations of (c) Mg<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> and (d) Ca<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> in the AIMD simulation at 300 K for 10 ps and the corresponding structure snapshots of (e) Mg<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> and (f) Ca<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small>.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
      
      
        
        <h3 id="sect746"><span class="b_heading">3.3 Electronic properties of Mg<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> and Ca<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> monolayers</span></h3>
        <span>The intrinsic electronic conductivity has a profound effect on the cyclability and rate performance of the anode materials. Hence, we first calculated the PBE band structures and density of states (DOS) for Mg<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> and Ca<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> monolayers to investigate their electrical behaviors. In <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig4">Fig. 4</a>, the Mg<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> and Ca<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> monolayers exhibit metallic behaviors with electronic bands passing through the Fermi level and notable peaks of total DOS at the Fermi energy, which indicates their good electronic conductivities. The projected DOS of Mg<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> shows that the B orbitals contribute dominantly while the Mg orbitals contribute partially near the Fermi level. For Ca<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small>, the DOS is jointly contributed by the Ca and B orbitals. Besides, the HSE06 bands and DOS show that there is no band gap for Mg<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> and Ca<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> as shown in Fig. S9.<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a> With excellent electronic conductivities, the Mg<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> and Ca<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> monolayers have the potential for applications as anode materials for LIBs and NIBs compared with the semi-conducting or insulating anode materials.</span>
        <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig4"><a href="/image/article/2024/NR/d4nr01786b/d4nr01786b-f4_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d4nr01786b-f4.tif" src="/image/article/2024/NR/d4nr01786b/d4nr01786b-f4.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 4 </b> <span id="fig4"><span class="graphic_title">Projected electronic band structures and DOS of (a) Mg<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> and (b) Ca<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> monolayers.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
      
      
        
        <h3 id="sect782"><span class="b_heading">3.4 Li/Na adsorption and storage capacities of Mg<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> and Ca<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> monolayers</span></h3>
        <span>Three adsorption sites were identified for the hexagonal Mg<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> and Ca<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> monolayers, including the hollow (S1), bridge (S2) and top (S3) sites, as shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig5">Fig. 5a</a>. The S1 site is positioned above the B atom corresponding to the hollow site of the triangle of M atoms. The S2 site is located at the top of the BB or MM bridges. And the S3 site is above the M atom. Utilizing <a title="" href="#eqn3">eqn (3)</a>, we calculated the single atom adsorption energies (<span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>ad</sub></small>) for Li and Na on these sites, as shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig5">Fig. 5b</a>. The single atom <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>ad</sub></small> of Li and Na on S1 are both the lowest for the Mg<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> (0.287 and 0.533 eV) and Ca<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> (0.102 and 0.477 eV) monolayers, indicating that the S1 is the most favorable adsorption site. All the most stable configurations are exhibited in Fig. S10.<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a> Besides, the single atom <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>ad</sub></small> of Na are lower than those of Li at the same site of Mg<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> or Ca<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small>, suggesting that the single Na atom adsorption configurations are more stable than those of Li. Furthermore, the single Li/Na atom <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>ad</sub></small> of Mg<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> is lower than Ca<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small>, indicating that the adsorption configuration of Mg<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> for a single Li/Na atom is more stable than that of Ca<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small>.</span>
        <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig5"><a href="/image/article/2024/NR/d4nr01786b/d4nr01786b-f5_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d4nr01786b-f5.tif" src="/image/article/2024/NR/d4nr01786b/d4nr01786b-f5.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 5 </b> <span id="fig5"><span class="graphic_title">(a) The considered adsorption sites on the surfaces of Mg<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> and Ca<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> monolayers and (b) the corresponding adsorption energies for Li and Na atoms.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
        <p class="otherpara">After identifying the most favorable adsorption site, we focused on determining the specific capacity of the Mg<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> and Ca<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> monolayers. Charging or discharging in LIBs and SIBs involves a gradual process of ion adsorption or release. To determine the adsorption configurations at different ion concentrations, we first investigated the average adsorption energies (<span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>ave</sub></small>) of the configurations with one layer of Li/Na on both sides. There are 8 hollow (S1) sites on one side of the monolayers. To determine the maximum numbers of Li/Na atoms that the monolayers can accommodate in one layer, we constructed two types of configurations adsorbed with different numbers of atoms. In <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig6">Fig. 6</a>, the Li<small><sub>2</sub></small>/Na<small><sub>2</sub></small>M<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> (M = Mg, Ca) configurations have 4 adsorbed atoms at S1 alternatively on each side of the monolayers. In Fig. S11,<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a> all 16 hollow sites of the monolayers are adsorbed with atoms in the Li<small><sub>4</sub></small>/Na<small><sub>4</sub></small>M<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> configurations. The calculated <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>ave</sub></small> of Li<small><sub>4</sub></small>/Na<small><sub>4</sub></small>M<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> and Li<small><sub>2</sub></small>/Na<small><sub>2</sub></small>M<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> according to <a title="" href="#eqn4">eqn (4)</a> are listed in Table S4.<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a> All Li<small><sub>4</sub></small>/Na<small><sub>4</sub></small>M<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> configurations have positive <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>ave</sub></small>, which means the monolayers cannot adsorb Li/Na atoms at all hollow sites. Conversely, the <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>ave</sub></small> of all Li<small><sub>2</sub></small>/Na<small><sub>2</sub></small>M<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> configurations are negative indicating that the monolayers can accommodate 8 atoms on hollow sites in one layer alternately. To analyze the adsorption configurations from the perspective of crystallography, we compared the (0 0 1) crystallographic planes of the monolayers with the (1 1 0) crystal planes of body-centered cubic Li and Na as plot in Fig. S12.<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a> In Fig. S12,<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a> to distinguish different hollow sites (S1) on the surface of Mg<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> and Ca<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small>, we labeled the S1, the nearest S1 and the next nearest neighbor S1 as A, B and C in Fig. S12,<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a> respectively. The distances between S1 and the next nearest neighbor S1 (<span class="italic">d</span><small><sub>AC</sub></small>) of Mg<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> and Ca<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> are 3.078 and 3.333 , respectively. While the distances between S1 and the nearest neighbor S1 (<span class="italic">d</span><small><sub>AB</sub></small>) of Mg<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> and Ca<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> are 1.777 and 1.924 , respectively. For body-centered cubic Li and Na, the distances between the body-centered atom and the nearest atom in corner (<span class="italic">d</span><small><sub>bc</sub></small>) in the (1 1 0) crystallographic planes are 2.996 and 3.638 , respectively. The mismatching ratios between <span class="italic">d</span><small><sub>AC</sub></small> and <span class="italic">d</span><small><sub>bc</sub></small> (<span class="italic">r</span><small><sub>1</sub></small>) and the ones between <span class="italic">d</span><small><sub>AB</sub></small> and <span class="italic">d</span><small><sub>bc</sub></small> (<span class="italic">r</span><small><sub>2</sub></small>) are listed in Tables S5 and S6,<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a> respectively. The absolute values of <span class="italic">r</span><small><sub>1</sub></small> are smaller than the ones of <span class="italic">r</span><small><sub>2</sub></small>. In other words, <span class="italic">d</span><small><sub>AC</sub></small> have a higher matching degree with <span class="italic">d</span><small><sub>bc</sub></small>, which indicates the monolayers undergo a smaller change in bond length by adsorbing the Li/Na atoms on hollow sites alternately instead of all hollow sites in one layer. In terms of the electronic structure, the charge difference densities of Li<small><sub>2</sub></small>/Na<small><sub>2</sub></small>M<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> configurations (in Fig. S13<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>) reveal that the electrons accumulate between the M atom layers of the monolayers and the Li/Na atom layers leading to the Li/NaM bonds. The strong chemical bonds between the Li/Na atoms and the monolayers prevent the formation of Li/Na clusters, which is beneficial to the applications of these monolayers in anode materials.</p>
        <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig6"><a href="/image/article/2024/NR/d4nr01786b/d4nr01786b-f6_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d4nr01786b-f6.tif" src="/image/article/2024/NR/d4nr01786b/d4nr01786b-f6.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 6 </b> <span id="fig6"><span class="graphic_title">Configurations of (a) Li<small><sub>2</sub></small>Mg<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small>, (b) Li<small><sub>2</sub></small>Ca<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small>, (c)&nbsp;Na<small><sub>2</sub></small>Mg<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small>&nbsp;and (d) Na<small><sub>2</sub></small>Ca<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> for the most stable sites. The Mg<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> and Ca<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> monolayers both tend to accommodate 8 atoms on hollow sites in one layer alternately.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
        <p class="otherpara">Based on the most favorable adsorption configurations for one-layer Li/Na atoms, we constructed a series of Li<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>/Na<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>M<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> configurations where <span class="italic">x</span> ranged from 0.25 to 3 in increments of 0.25 and calculated the corresponding <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>ave</sub></small> using <a title="" href="#eqn4">eqn (4)</a>. The results of <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>ave</sub></small> are presented in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig7">Fig. 7</a>. For Li<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>Ca<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> and Li<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>Mg<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small>, the <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>ave</sub></small> are all negative values in 0 &lt; <span class="italic">x</span> &lt; 2 and two consecutive positive values appear at <span class="italic">x</span> = 2.25 and 2.5. The maximum Li atom concentrations for Mg<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> and Ca<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> are both <span class="italic">x</span> = 2 at which the adsorption configurations remain energetically stable. According to <a title="" href="#eqn5">eqn (5)</a>, the specific capacities of the hexagonal Mg<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> and Ca<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> monolayers for Li atoms are 764 and 527 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small>, respectively. Their capacities significantly exceed those of graphene,<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit18"><sup><span class="sup_ref">18</span></sup></a> MXenes<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit13"><sup><span class="sup_ref">13,28,54</span></sup></a> and most MBenes<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit30"><sup><span class="sup_ref">30,35,36,52</span></sup></a> due to their much smaller molecular weights. For Na<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>Ca<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> and Na<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>Mg<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small>, no positive adsorption energies occur in <span class="italic">x</span> &lt; 2, while continuous values close to zero occurs in <span class="italic">x</span> &gt; 2. Specifically, the <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>ave</sub></small> of Na<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>Mg<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> at <span class="italic">x</span> = 2.5 and 2.75 are 0.080 and 0.057 eV, respectively, and the <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>ave</sub></small> of Na<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>Ca<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> at <span class="italic">x</span> = 2.25 and 2.5 are 0.003 and 0.067 eV, respectively. These values in the range of 0.1 to 0 eV indicate that the corresponding configurations are energetically unstable.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit35"><sup><span class="sup_ref">35,36</span></sup></a> As a result, the 2  2  1 Mg<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> and Ca<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> can accommodate 9 and 8 Na atoms corresponding to the Na<small><sub>2.25</sub></small>Mg<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> and Na<small><sub>2</sub></small>Ca<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> configurations, respectively. Consequently, the specific capacities of the hexagonal Mg<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> and Ca<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> monolayers are 859 and 527 mA h g<small><sup>1</sup></small>, respectively, surpassing the capacities of recently reported MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small>,<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit25"><sup><span class="sup_ref">25</span></sup></a> WB<small><sub>4</sub></small>,<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit55"><sup><span class="sup_ref">55</span></sup></a> Nb<small><sub>2</sub></small>Se<small><sub>2</sub></small>C,<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit56"><sup><span class="sup_ref">56</span></sup></a> all MXenes<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit13"><sup><span class="sup_ref">13,29</span></sup></a> and most MBenes.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit36"><sup><span class="sup_ref">36,37,52,53</span></sup></a> Notably, we observed that the <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>ave</sub></small> values of Li<small><sub>3</sub></small>Mg<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small>, Li<small><sub>3</sub></small>Ca<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> and Na<small><sub>3</sub></small>Ca<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> configurations are negative. Nevertheless, it is essential to recognize that charging is a gradual and continuous process of ions on anodes. Consequently, configurations at <span class="italic">x</span> = 3 cannot form since the charging processes terminate at <span class="italic">x</span> = 2.</p>
        <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig7"><a href="/image/article/2024/NR/d4nr01786b/d4nr01786b-f7_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d4nr01786b-f7.tif" src="/image/article/2024/NR/d4nr01786b/d4nr01786b-f7.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 7 </b> <span id="fig7"><span class="graphic_title">Average adsorption energies (<span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>ave</sub></small>) of the Li<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>/Na<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>M<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> configurations at different Li/Na concentrations (<span class="italic">x</span>, from 0 to 3 by step 0.25). The configurations with values of <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>ave</sub></small> in the range of 0.1 to 0 eV are considered to be energetically unstable.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
        <p class="otherpara">Small bond length changes, cross-sectional area change ratios and volume change ratios are important properties for 2D electrode materials. The structural schematics of the monolayers adsorbed with one layer of ions on both sides are displayed in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig8">Fig. 8a</a>. The distances between the ion layers and the monolayers (<span class="italic">d</span><small><sub>1</sub></small>), the thicknesses of the monolayers (<span class="italic">d</span><small><sub>2</sub></small>) and the MM, BB and MB bond lengths (<span class="italic">l</span><small><sub>MM</sub></small>, <span class="italic">l</span><small><sub>BB</sub></small>, <span class="italic">l</span><small><sub>MB</sub></small>) have small changes of approximately 10<small><sup>2</sup></small>  in magnitude from the M<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> to the Li/NaM<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> or Li<small><sub>2</sub></small>/Na<small><sub>2</sub></small>M<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> configurations. All these values of the distances are listed in Table S7.<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a> Besides, the histograms of the <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>ave</sub></small> and the corresponding cross-section area (<span class="italic">S</span><small><sub>cross</sub></small>) change ratios of the monolayers with one layer of ions adsorbed on the only top side (Li/NaM<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small>) and one layer of ions adsorbed on both sides (Li<small><sub>2</sub></small>/Na<small><sub>2</sub></small>M<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small>) are plotted in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig8">Fig. 8b</a>. Among these configurations, Na<small><sub>2</sub></small>Mg<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> possesses the highest <span class="italic">S</span><small><sub>cross</sub></small> change ratio which is not exceeding 4.9%. These results demonstrate that the bond length changes and the <span class="italic">S</span><small><sub>cross</sub></small> changes of Mg<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> and Ca<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> monolayers are very small, meeting the requirements of electrode materials. Furthermore, apart from the cross-section area change ratio, we also investigated the volume change ratio (<span class="italic"></span>) of Mg<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> and Ca<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> at different Li/Na concentrations. The results are shown in the Fig. S14 in the ESI.<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a> In Fig. S14,<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a> the Mg<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> and Ca<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> exhibit volume expansion and reduction at different Li/Na loading stages without obvious trend. The absolute values of <span class="italic"></span> of Li<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>/Na<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>Mg<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> (<span class="italic">x</span>, 0.25 to 2 with the increment of 0.25) are less than 2.5% in the whole charging or discharging process. The maximum of the absolute values of <span class="italic"></span> of Li<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>Ca<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> and Na<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>Ca<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> are 4.3% (<span class="italic">x</span> = 1) and 6.3% (<span class="italic">x</span> = 0.75), respectively, which are comparable to the transition metal MBene anode materials (&lt;7%).<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit34"><sup><span class="sup_ref">34</span></sup></a> The small volume changes observed at different ion loading stages further demonstrate the structural stabilities of Mg<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> and Ca<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> monolayers.</p>
        <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig8"><a href="/image/article/2024/NR/d4nr01786b/d4nr01786b-f8_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d4nr01786b-f8.tif" src="/image/article/2024/NR/d4nr01786b/d4nr01786b-f8.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 8 </b> <span id="fig8"><span class="graphic_title">(a) Structural schematics of the Li<small><sub>2</sub></small>/Na<small><sub>2</sub></small>M<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> configurations and (b) the histograms of the average adsorption energies (<span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>ave</sub></small>) and the corresponding cross-section area (<span class="italic">S</span><small><sub>cross</sub></small>) change ratios of the Li/NaM<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> and Li<small><sub>2</sub></small>/Na<small><sub>2</sub></small>M<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> configurations.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
        <p class="otherpara">To evaluate the influence of mechanical properties on the anode performance, we calculated the Young's modulus (<span class="italic">E</span>), shear modulus (<span class="italic">G</span>) and Poisson's ratio (<span class="italic"></span>) of Mg<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> and Ca<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> and compared them with other hexagonal MBenes. The results of <span class="italic">E</span>, <span class="italic">G</span> and <span class="italic"></span> can be found in Fig. S3, S4 and Table S1 in the ESI.<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a> Mg<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> and Ca<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> exhibit high <span class="italic">E</span> and <span class="italic">G</span> (141 and 60 N m<small><sup>1</sup></small>, 114 and 51 N m<small><sup>1</sup></small> respectively) and low <span class="italic"></span> (0.17 and 0.11 respectively) indicating their excellent mechanical stabilities and processing properties. Despite possessing lower <span class="italic">E</span> and <span class="italic">G</span> values than transition metal MBenes such as Ti<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> (234 and 101 N m<small><sup>1</sup></small>) and Zr<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> (230 and 97 N m<small><sup>1</sup></small>), Mg<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> and Ca<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> as strong as MoS<small><sub>2</sub></small> (124 and 50 N m<small><sup>1</sup></small>)<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit57"><sup><span class="sup_ref">57</span></sup></a> still meet the performance requirements for anode materials.</p>
      
      
        
        <h3 id="sect1180"><span class="b_heading">3.5 Electronic structures of Mg<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> and Ca<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> monolayers adsorbed with Li/Na ions</span></h3>
        <span>The conductivity during the charge or discharge process has a great influence on the rate performance of the anode materials. To comprehensively understand the electrical conductivity during charging and discharging, we investigated the evolutions of the elemental projected DOS for the monolayers as shown in Fig. S15 and S16.<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a> For Li<small><sub>0.25</sub></small>Mg<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> (Fig. S15a<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>) and Li<small><sub>2</sub></small>Mg<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> (Fig. S15b<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>), the B-p orbitals contribute dominantly at Fermi level while the contributions of the other orbitals are neglectable. Hence, Li atoms have a weak effect on the electronic structure of Li<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>Mg<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small>. From Na<small><sub>0.25</sub></small>Mg<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> (Fig. S15c<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>) to Na<small><sub>2</sub></small>Mg<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> (Fig. S15d<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>), the dominance of the B-p orbitals remains with a slight increase in the contribution of Na-s orbitals, which is comparable to that of the Mg-p orbitals. From Li<small><sub>0.25</sub></small>Ca<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> (Fig. S16a<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>) to Li<small><sub>2</sub></small>Ca<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> (Fig. S16b<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>), the orbital contributions at the Fermi level change from the dominant contribution of B-p orbitals to the coupling contribution of Ca-p and B-p orbitals as the number of Li atoms increases. While the Li-s orbitals are negligible in the orbital contributions. For Na<small><sub>0.25</sub></small>Ca<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> (Fig. S16c<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>), the B-p orbitals contribute dominantly at the Fermi level. For Na<small><sub>2</sub></small>Ca<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> (Fig. S16b<a title="Select to navigate to footnote" href="#fn1"></a>), the Na-s, Ca-p and B-p orbitals make the coupling contribution while the contribution of B-p orbitals reduces a lot comparing to Na<small><sub>0.25</sub></small>Ca<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small>. Hence, the electronic structure of Ca<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> is more sensitive to Li/Na atoms than Mg<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small>. In general, these systems both remain metallic behaviors at low and high concentrations of Li/Na atoms, thereby exhibiting excellent electrical conductivities as anode materials in LIBs or SIBs.</span>
      
      
        
        <h3 id="sect1232"><span class="b_heading">3.6 Theoretical open-circuit voltages of Mg<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> and Ca<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> monolayers</span></h3>
        <span>A good anode material should possess low OCVs ranged from 0 to 1 V, which prevent the anode from forming dendrites during charge or discharge and provide a large voltage window to match the cathode.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit16"><sup><span class="sup_ref">16</span></sup></a> To acquire the OCV profiles of the Mg<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> and Ca<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> monolayers, we calculated the formation energies (<span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>f</sub></small>) of Li<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>/Na<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>M<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> configurations (0  <span class="italic">x</span>  3) using <a title="" href="#eqn6">eqn (6)</a> and constructed the convex hull of <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>f</sub></small>. The convex hulls of <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>f</sub></small> for Li<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>Mg<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> and Na<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>Mg<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> are exhibited in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig9">Fig. 9a and b</a>, respectively. Blue points located on the convex hull correspond to the configurations more thermodynamically stable than others. The red points correspond to the metastable configurations. All the points corresponding to the configurations with positive <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>ave</sub></small> are excluded in the convex hulls of <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>f</sub></small>. For Li<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>Mg<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small>, the configurations corresponding to <span class="italic">x</span> = 0.25, 1.0 and 2.0 are more stable than the other configurations. For Na<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>Mg<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small>, the configurations at <span class="italic">x</span> = 0.25, 1.25, 2.0 and 2.25 are more stable. According to the <a title="" href="#eqn7">eqn (7)</a>, the OCV plateaus between two adjacent stable configurations were calculated with the corresponding energy differences between them. The OCV profiles of Li<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>Mg<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> and Na<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>Mg<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> are exhibited in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig9">Fig. 9</a>. In <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig9">Fig. 9c</a>, the three voltage plateaus for stable Li<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>Mg<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> adsorption configurations (<span class="italic">x</span>  2) are 0.287, 0.436 and 0.463 V corresponding to Mg<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small>  Li<small><sub>0.25</sub></small>Mg<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small>, Li<small><sub>0.25</sub></small>Mg<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small>  Li<small><sub>1.0</sub></small>Mg<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> and Li<small><sub>1.0</sub></small>Mg<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small>  Li<small><sub>2.0</sub></small>Mg<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small>, respectively. However, the plateau corresponding to Li<small><sub>2.0</sub></small>Mg<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small>  Li<small><sub>3.0</sub></small>Mg<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> cannot form due to the unstable adsorption configurations in 2 &lt; <span class="italic">x</span> &lt; 3. The average OCV for Li<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>Mg<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> is 0.395 V <span class="italic">vs.</span> Li/Li<small><sup>+</sup></small>. In <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig9">Fig. 9d</a>, Na<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>Mg<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> has four OCV plateaus in 0  <span class="italic">x</span>  2.25, including 0.532, 0.240, 0.230 and 0.114 V, and the average OCV of these four plateaus is 0.279 V <span class="italic">vs.</span> Na/Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>. These results indicate that Mg<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> is a promising anode material providing low OCVs and large voltage windows for LIBs and SIBs. The convex hulls of <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>f</sub></small> and corresponding OCV profiles for Li<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>Ca<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> and Na<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>Ca<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> are displayed in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig10">Fig. 10</a>. For Li<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>Ca<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small>, two relatively stable configurations (<span class="italic">x</span> = 1.0 and 2.0) lead to two OCV plateaus (0.266 and 0.284 V) within the negative <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>ave</sub></small> range (<span class="italic">x</span>  2) in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig10">Fig. 10c</a>. For Na<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>Ca<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small>, the stable configurations at <span class="italic">x</span> = 0.25, 0.5, 1.25, 1.5 and 2.0 result in five OCV plateaus (0.476, 0.375, 0.318, 0.326 and 0.315 V) within the negative <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>ave</sub></small> range (<span class="italic">x</span>  2) in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig10">Fig. 10d</a>. The average OCVs of Li<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>Ca<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> and Na<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>Ca<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> are 0.275 V <span class="italic">vs.</span> Li/Li<small><sup>+</sup></small> and 0.362 V <span class="italic">vs.</span> Na/Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>, respectively. The initial and average potentials of Ca<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> are quite low making it suitable for anode materials in LIBs or SIBs.</span>
        <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig9"><a href="/image/article/2024/NR/d4nr01786b/d4nr01786b-f9_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d4nr01786b-f9.tif" src="/image/article/2024/NR/d4nr01786b/d4nr01786b-f9.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 9 </b> <span id="fig9"><span class="graphic_title">The convex hulls of <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>f</sub></small> for (a) Li<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>Mg<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> and (b) Na<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>Mg<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> configurations. Blue points located on the convex hull (blue lines) represent the stable adsorption configurations against the other configurations. The metastable configurations are indicated by red points. The concertation points corresponding to positive <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>ave</sub></small> are excluded. OCV profiles of (c) Li<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>Mg<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> against Li/Li<small><sup>+</sup></small> and (d) Na<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>Mg<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> against Na/Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>. The average OCVs in the concentration range corresponding to negative <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>ave</sub></small> are labeled under the profiles.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
        <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig10"><a href="/image/article/2024/NR/d4nr01786b/d4nr01786b-f10_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: d4nr01786b-f10.tif" src="/image/article/2024/NR/d4nr01786b/d4nr01786b-f10.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 10 </b> <span id="fig10"><span class="graphic_title">The convex hulls of <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>f</sub></small> for (a) Li<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>Ca<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> and (b) Na<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>Ca<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> configurations. Blue points located on the convex hull (blue lines) represent the stable adsorption configurations against the other configurations. The metastable configurations are indicated by red points. The concentration points corresponding to positive <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>ave</sub></small> are excluded. OCV profiles of (c) Li<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>Ca<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> against Li/Li<small><sup>+</sup></small> and (d) Na<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>Ca<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> against Na/Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>. The average OCVs in the concentration ranges corresponding to negative <span class="italic">E</span><small><sub>ave</sub></small> are labeled under the profiles.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
      
      
        
        <h3 id="sect1433"><span class="b_heading">3.7 Li/Na diffusion on Mg<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> and Ca<small><sub>2</sub></small>B<small><sub>2</sub></small> monolayers</span></h3>
